font-size: 88%">West Indian manatee range\n</td></tr>\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n</tbody></table><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1238732961">@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:transparent}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}}.mw-parser-output .infobox.biota .taxobox-edit-taxonomy img{background:transparent!important}body.skin-vector .mw-parser-output table.biota.infobox{margin-top:0.5em}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output table.biota.infobox tr.taxonrow td{padding:2px 10px}</style>\n<p>The <b>West Indian manatee</b> (<i><b>Trichechus manatus</b></i>), also known as the <b>North American manatee</b>, is a large, aquatic mammal native to warm coastal areas of the Caribbean, from the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_United_States" title="Eastern United States">Eastern United States</a> to northern Brazil. Living alone or in herds, it feeds on underwater plants and uses its whiskers to navigate. It is divided into two subspecies, the <b>Florida manatee</b> (<i>T. m. latirostris</i>) in the United States and the <b>Antillean manatee</b> (<i>T. m. manatus</i>) in the Caribbean,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> both of which are endangered and face pressure from habitat loss, pollution, and other human activity. The West Indian manatee is the largest living member of the <a href="/wiki/Sirenia" title="Sirenia">sirenians</a> (order Sirenia), a group of large aquatic mammals that includes the <a href="/wiki/Dugong" title="Dugong">dugong</a>, other <a href="/wiki/Manatee" title="Manatee">manatees</a>, and the extinct <a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_sea_cow" title="Steller&#39;s sea cow">Steller\'s sea cow</a>.\n</p><p>Manatees are <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a>, have developed vocal communication abilities, and are covered in highly sensitive whiskers (called <a href="/wiki/Vibrissae" class="mw-redirect" title="Vibrissae">vibrissae</a>) that are used for feeding and navigation.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaspard22_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaspard22-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In breeding season, several males form mating herds around an individual female; on average, one calf is born to a female manatee every two to three years.<sup id="cite_ref-South_Florida_Multi-1999_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Florida_Multi-1999-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1970s, the West Indian manatee was listed as "endangered" in the United States under the <a href="/wiki/Endangered_Species_Act_of_1973" title="Endangered Species Act of 1973">Endangered Species Act</a>, when there were only several hundred left.<sup id="cite_ref-smithsonianmag_201722_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smithsonianmag_201722-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The decades since have witnessed significant efforts to protect this species from natural and human threats, particularly collisions with <a href="/wiki/Watercraft" title="Watercraft">watercraft</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2017, the United States changed the classification to "threatened", citing a substantial increase in the total population<i>.<sup id="cite_ref-smithsonianmag_201722_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smithsonianmag_201722-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fws_manatee_threatened_201733_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fws_manatee_threatened_201733-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></i>\n</p>\n<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Description">Description</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>The average West Indian manatee is about 2.7\xe2\x80\x933.5&#160;m (8.9\xe2\x80\x9311.5&#160;ft) long and weighs 200\xe2\x80\x93600&#160;kg (440\xe2\x80\x931,320&#160;lb), with females generally larger than males.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The largest individual on record weighed 1,655&#160;kg (3,649&#160;lb) and measured 4.6&#160;m (15&#160;ft) long.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Manatees are estimated to live 50 years or more in the wild,<sup id="cite_ref-South_Florida_Multi-1999_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Florida_Multi-1999-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and one captive Florida manatee, <a href="/wiki/Snooty" title="Snooty">Snooty</a>, lived for 69 years (1948\xe2\x80\x932017).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:West_Indian_Manatee_Skull.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/West_Indian_Manatee_Skull.jpg/220px-West_Indian_Manatee_Skull.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/West_Indian_Manatee_Skull.jpg/330px-West_Indian_Manatee_Skull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/West_Indian_Manatee_Skull.jpg/440px-West_Indian_Manatee_Skull.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a><figcaption>Skull of a West Indian manatee on display at <a href="/wiki/The_Museum_of_Osteology" class="mw-redirect" title="The Museum of Osteology">The Museum of Osteology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_City,_Oklahoma" class="mw-redirect" title="Oklahoma City, Oklahoma">Oklahoma City, Oklahoma</a></figcaption></figure>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span class="mw-3d-wrapper" data-label="3D"><a href="/wiki/File:Trichecus_manatus_3d_scan_Natural_History_Museum_University_of_Pisa_C_1442.stl" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Trichecus_manatus_3d_scan_Natural_History_Museum_University_of_Pisa_C_1442.stl/220px-Trichecus_manatus_3d_scan_Natural_History_Museum_University_of_Pisa_C_1442.stl.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Trichecus_manatus_3d_scan_Natural_History_Museum_University_of_Pisa_C_1442.stl/330px-Trichecus_manatus_3d_scan_Natural_History_Museum_University_of_Pisa_C_1442.stl.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Trichecus_manatus_3d_scan_Natural_History_Museum_University_of_Pisa_C_1442.stl/440px-Trichecus_manatus_3d_scan_Natural_History_Museum_University_of_Pisa_C_1442.stl.png 2x" data-file-width="5120" data-file-height="2880" /></a></span><figcaption>3D model of skeleton</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Given that manatees are <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, they breathe air, have warm blood, and produce milk. Like the other <a href="/wiki/Sirenian" class="mw-redirect" title="Sirenian">sirenians</a>, the West Indian manatee has adapted fully to aquatic life, having no hind limbs. Instead of hind limbs, the manatee has a spatula-like paddle for propulsion in the water. Manatees have evolved streamlined bodies which lack external ear flaps, thus decreasing resistance in the aquatic environment. <a href="/wiki/Pelage" class="mw-redirect" title="Pelage">Pelage</a> cover is sparsely distributed across the body, which may play a role in reducing the build-up of <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> on their thick skin. Manatee skin is gray but can vary in coloration due to algae and other biota, like <a href="/wiki/Barnacle" title="Barnacle">barnacles</a>, that opportunistically live on manatees.  Scar tissue on manatees is white and persists for decades, allowing for easy identification. The Florida manatee has three to four nails on each flipper.<sup id="cite_ref-\xe2\x80\x9creep2006\xe2\x80\x9d2_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-\xe2\x80\x9creep2006\xe2\x80\x9d2-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The West Indian manatee has a prehensile snout, like their relative the <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephant</a>, for grabbing vegetation and bringing it into their mouths. Manatees have six to eight <a href="/wiki/Molar_(tooth)" title="Molar (tooth)">molariform teeth</a> in each jaw quadrant. These molariform teeth are generated at the back of the mouth and slowly migrate towards the front of the mouth, at a rate of 1\xe2\x80\x932&#160;mm per month, where they then fall out. This tooth \xe2\x80\x98conveyor belt\xe2\x80\x99 provides unlimited tooth production which is  beneficial for the manatee which feeds on vegetation four to eight hours per day and consumes 5-10% of its body weight per day. Manatees have 3\xe2\x80\x935&#160;cm hairs that cover their whole body and provide <a href="/wiki/Somatosensory_system" title="Somatosensory system">somatosensory information</a>. Manatee bones are dense and solid which allows them to act as <a href="/wiki/Ballast" title="Ballast">ballast</a> and promote negative buoyancy. This helps counteract the positive buoyancy which comes from their high fat content. These two buoyancy counterparts, along with air in the lungs, helps manatees achieve neutral buoyancy in the water.  This makes breathing, foraging, and swimming easier for the manatee. Manatees are unique, compared to other mammals, in that they have a longitudinally oriented <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm">diaphragm</a> that is split in half to form two hemidiaphragms. Each hemidiaphragm is capable of independent muscular contractions.<sup id="cite_ref-\xe2\x80\x9creep2006\xe2\x80\x9d2_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-\xe2\x80\x9creep2006\xe2\x80\x9d2-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>The West Indian manatee inhabits mostly shallow coastal areas, including <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a>.  Manatees can withstand large changes in <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> and are found in both freshwater and saltwater.<sup id="cite_ref-South_Florida_Multi-1999_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Florida_Multi-1999-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Manatees\' extremely low metabolic rate and lack of a thick layer of <a href="/wiki/Blubber" title="Blubber">insulating body fat</a> limits them to locations with warm waters, including tropical regions.\n</p><p>The Florida subspecies is primarily found along the Florida coast, but has been spotted as far north as <a href="/wiki/Dennis,_Massachusetts" title="Dennis, Massachusetts">Dennis, Massachusetts</a>, as well as along the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Coast_of_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Gulf Coast of Texas">Gulf Coast of Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Concerning their forays inland, a manatee was once spotted in the <a href="/wiki/Wolf_River_(Tennessee)" title="Wolf River (Tennessee)">Wolf River</a> (near where it enters the Mississippi) in <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis, Tennessee</a>, in 2006: a distance of over 700 river miles from the Gulf of Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Antillean subspecies has a sparse distribution throughout the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, ranging as far north as <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> and as far south as <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vianna_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vianna-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>An analysis of <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> patterns indicates there are actually three primary geographic groups of the West Indian manatee: (i) Florida and the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Antilles" title="Greater Antilles">Greater Antilles</a>; (ii) Mexico, <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a> and northern South America; and (iii) northeastern South America.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vianna_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vianna-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The seasonal distribution of the West Indian manatee varies with water temperatures. Temperatures below approximately 20&#160;\xc2\xb0C (68&#160;\xc2\xb0F) increase the risk of cold-induced stress or mortality for manatees.<sup id="cite_ref-South_Florida_Multi-1999_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Florida_Multi-1999-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-haubold_20072_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haubold_20072-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Consequently, Florida manatees seek out warm-water refuges during the winter, concentrated primarily along the Florida peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Many of these refuges are artificial, created by the runoff from nearby power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In contrast, the Antillean manatee is less susceptible to cold-induced stress due to the warmer waters across its range.\n</p>\n<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Manatee_Florida.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Manatee_Florida.jpg/220px-Manatee_Florida.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="179" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Manatee_Florida.jpg/330px-Manatee_Florida.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Manatee_Florida.jpg/440px-Manatee_Florida.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="520" /></a><figcaption>Manatee from <a href="/wiki/Crystal_River,_Florida" title="Crystal River, Florida">Crystal River, Florida</a></figcaption></figure>\n<p>Florida manatees inhabit the most northern limit of sirenian habitats. There are four recognized subpopulations of the Florida manatee, termed the Northwest, Southwest, Atlantic Coast and <a href="/wiki/St._Johns_River" title="St. Johns River">St. John\'s River</a> populations.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Large concentrations of Florida manatees are located in the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_River,_Florida" title="Crystal River, Florida">Crystal River</a><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Blue_Spring_State_Park" title="Blue Spring State Park">Blue Springs</a> regions in central and north Florida. The Antillean manatee is sparsely distributed throughout the Caribbean and the northwestern Atlantic Ocean, from <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, east to the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Antilles" title="Greater Antilles">Greater Antilles</a>, and south to <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>. Populations can also be found in <a href="/wiki/The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas">The Bahamas</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trinidad" title="Trinidad">Trinidad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cura%C3%A7ao" title="Cura\xc3\xa7ao">Cura\xc3\xa7ao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>. It is possibly <a href="/wiki/Extirpated" class="mw-redirect" title="Extirpated">extirpated</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Turks_and_Caicos_Islands" title="Turks and Caicos Islands">Turks and Caicos Islands</a>, and definitively so from all the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Antilles" title="Lesser Antilles">Lesser Antilles</a> except Trinidad and Cura\xc3\xa7ao. Its presence in <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> is uncertain.<sup id="cite_ref-fws_manatee_threatened_201733_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fws_manatee_threatened_201733-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Reintroduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Reintroduction">reintroduction</a> was planned to take place in <a href="/wiki/Guadeloupe" title="Guadeloupe">Guadeloupe</a>, but was terminated early due to political issues.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Behavior_and_diet">Behavior and diet</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Behavior and diet"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Manatees_at_Haulover_Canal.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Manatees_at_Haulover_Canal.jpg/220px-Manatees_at_Haulover_Canal.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Manatees_at_Haulover_Canal.jpg/330px-Manatees_at_Haulover_Canal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Manatees_at_Haulover_Canal.jpg/440px-Manatees_at_Haulover_Canal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2715" data-file-height="1843" /></a><figcaption>Basking at <a href="/wiki/Haulover_Canal" title="Haulover Canal">Haulover Canal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merritt_Island_National_Wildlife_Refuge" title="Merritt Island National Wildlife Refuge">Merritt Island National Wildlife Refuge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a></figcaption></figure>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Behavior">Behavior</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Behavior"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Because manatees evolved in habitats without natural predators, they lack predator avoidance behavior.  The large size and low <a href="/wiki/Basal_metabolic_rate" title="Basal metabolic rate">metabolic rates</a> of manatees lends to their capacity for long and deep dives, as well as their relative lack of speed. Manatees are frequently solitary creatures, but they do aggregate at warm-water habitats during the winter and during the formation of breeding herds.<sup id="cite_ref-South_Florida_Multi-1999_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Florida_Multi-1999-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In spite of their docile demeanor, due to their large size the West Indian manatee has nearly no natural predators in its native environment. <a href="/wiki/American_alligator" title="American alligator">American alligators</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_crocodile" title="American crocodile">American crocodiles</a> have been suspected to on occasion take a manatee.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A dying manatee was discovered in Puerto Rico, wounded by a large shark bite thought to be inflicted by either a <a href="/wiki/Tiger_shark" title="Tiger shark">tiger shark</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bull_shark" title="Bull shark">bull shark</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is projected that large sharks may threaten manatees occasionally but rarely enter the manatee\'s brackish habitats, whereas marine mammals in more direct coastal oceanic waters are well known to be highly vulnerable to sharks and <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orcas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Communication">Communication</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Communication"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Manatees have been shown to form large mating herds when males come across estrous females, indicating that males may be able to sense <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">estrogen</a> or other chemical indicators.<sup id="cite_ref-Learning_811-8252_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Learning_811-8252-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Manatees can communicate information to each other through their vocalization patterns.<sup id="cite_ref-2001_12_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2001_12-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sex and age-related differences are apparent in the vocalization structure of common squeaks and screeches in adult males, adult females, and juveniles.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Umeed-2018_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Umeed-2018-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This may be an indication of vocal individuality among manatees.<sup id="cite_ref-Umeed-2018_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Umeed-2018-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An increase in manatee vocalization after a vocal playback stimulus shows that they may be able to recognize another manatee\'s individual voice.<sup id="cite_ref-Umeed-2018_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Umeed-2018-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This behavior in manatees is found mostly between mother and calf interactions.<sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds-1981_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-1981-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, vocalization can still be commonly found in a variety of social interactions within groups of manatees, which is similar to other aquatic mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds-1981_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-1981-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> When communicating in noisy environments, manatees that are in groups experience the same <a href="/wiki/Lombard_effect" title="Lombard effect">Lombard effect</a> as humans do; where they will involuntary increase their <a href="/wiki/Vocal_effort" title="Vocal effort">vocal effort</a> when communicating in loud environments.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Based on acoustic and anatomical evidence, mammalian <a href="/wiki/Vocal_folds" class="mw-redirect" title="Vocal folds">vocal folds</a> are assumed to be the mechanism for sound production in manatees.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Manatees also eat other manatees\' feces; it is assumed that they do this to gather information about reproductive status or dominance, indicating the important role chemoreception plays in the social and reproductive behavior of manatees.<sup id="cite_ref-Learning_811-8252_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Learning_811-8252-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Diet">Diet</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Diet"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Manatees are obligate herbivores that feed on over 60 species of aquatic plants in both fresh and salt water. <a href="/wiki/Seagrass" title="Seagrass">Seagrass</a> is a staple of the manatee diet, particularly in coastal areas.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition, when the tide is high enough, they will also feed on grasses, roots and leaves (including those of <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangroves</a>), as well as algae.<sup id="cite_ref-Best-1981_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Best-1981-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sta.uwi.edu_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sta.uwi.edu-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-animaldiversity.org_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-animaldiversity.org-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Manatees typically graze for five or more hours per day, consuming anywhere from 4% to 10% of their body weight in wet vegetation per day, though the exact amount depends on their body size and activity level.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because manatees feed on abrasive plants, their <a href="/wiki/Molar_(tooth)" title="Molar (tooth)">molars</a> are often worn down and are replaced many times throughout their lives, thus earning the nickname "marching molars." The molar teeth are similar in shape, but of varying sizes. Manatees do not have incisors; these have been replaced by horny gingival plates.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some individuals may also inadvertently eat invertebrates (such as small aquatic insects and crustaceans) and will eat fish both in captivity and in the wild.<sup id="cite_ref-sta.uwi.edu_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sta.uwi.edu-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-animaldiversity.org_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-animaldiversity.org-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Manatees are nonruminants with an enlarged <a href="/wiki/Hindgut" title="Hindgut">hindgut</a>. Unlike other hindgut fermenters, such as the horse, manatees efficiently extract nutrients, particularly cellulose, from the aquatic plants in their diet. Manatees have a large gastrointestinal tract with contents measuring about 23% of its total body mass. In addition, the passage rate of food is very long (about seven days).<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This slow process increases the digestibility of their diet. It is suggested that chronic fermentation may also provide additional heat and is correlated with their low metabolic rate.<sup id="cite_ref-Best-1981_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Best-1981-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Vibrissae">Vibrissae</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Vibrissae"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sculpture_of_Manatee.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Sculpture_of_Manatee.JPG/220px-Sculpture_of_Manatee.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Sculpture_of_Manatee.JPG/330px-Sculpture_of_Manatee.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Sculpture_of_Manatee.JPG/440px-Sculpture_of_Manatee.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a><figcaption>Sculpture of manatee showing vibrissae</figcaption></figure>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hpim0279.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Hpim0279.jpg/220px-Hpim0279.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Hpim0279.jpg/330px-Hpim0279.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Hpim0279.jpg/440px-Hpim0279.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1952" /></a><figcaption>All the hairs of the manatee may be <a href="/wiki/Vibrissae" class="mw-redirect" title="Vibrissae">vibrissae</a></figcaption></figure>\n<p>Manatees have sensitive tactile hairs that cover their bodies and faces called <a href="/wiki/Whiskers" title="Whiskers">whiskers</a> or <i>vibrissae</i>. Each individual hair is a vibrissal apparatus called a follicle-sinus complex. Vibrissae are blood filled sinuses bound by a dense connective tissue capsule with sensitive nerve endings that provides haptic feedback to the manatee.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaspard22_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaspard22-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Usually vibrissae are found on the facial regions of terrestrial and non-sirenian aquatic animals and are called <a href="/wiki/Whiskers" title="Whiskers">whiskers</a>.  Manatees, however, have vibrissae all over their bodies. The vibrissae located in their facial region are roughly 30 times denser than the vibrissae on the rest of their body. Their mouth consists of very mobile <a href="/wiki/Prehensile" class="mw-redirect" title="Prehensile">prehensile</a> lips which are used for grasping food and objects. The vibrissae on these lips are turned outward during grasping and are used in locating vegetation. Their oral disks also contain vibrissae which have been classified as bristle-like hairs that are used in nongrasping investigation of objects and food.<sup id="cite_ref-Hartman-1979_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hartman-1979-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Manatee vibrissae are so sensitive that they are able to perform active touch discrimination of textures. Manatees also use their vibrissae to navigate the turbid waterways of their environment. Research has indicated that they are able to use these vibrissae to detect hydrodynamic stimuli in the same way that fish use their <a href="/wiki/Lateral_line" title="Lateral line">lateral line</a> system.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaspard22_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaspard22-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Reproduction">Reproduction</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Reproduction"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Manatee_mound_schnoot_1.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Manatee_mound_schnoot_1.png/220px-Manatee_mound_schnoot_1.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="85" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Manatee_mound_schnoot_1.png/330px-Manatee_mound_schnoot_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Manatee_mound_schnoot_1.png/440px-Manatee_mound_schnoot_1.png 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="2331" /></a><figcaption>A manatee mating herd near Leffis Key, Florida.</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Male manatees reach sexual maturity at 3\xe2\x80\x934 years of age, while females reach sexual maturity at 3\xe2\x80\x935 years of age.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-South_Florida_Multi-1999_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Florida_Multi-1999-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Manatees appear capable of breeding throughout their entire adult life, although most females first breed successfully at ages 7\xe2\x80\x939.  Breeding occurs in ephemeral mating herds, where several males aggregate around an estrous female and compete for access to her.<sup id="cite_ref-Hartman-1979_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hartman-1979-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These mating herds can last up to 2 to 4 weeks. The estrous cycles of female manatees typically are approximately one month long. Females in captivity have been observed to skip cycles.<sup id="cite_ref-Sheldon_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheldon-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Larger males, presumably older, have been observed to dominate mating herds and are likely responsible for most successful copulation events.<sup id="cite_ref-NBSIT_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NBSIT-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, between males and females there is a little sexual dimorphism where females are typically slightly larger than males.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The reproductive anatomy of males consists of internal testes within the abdominal cavity, which are located posterior to the kidneys. In the Florida manatee, males experience seasonal changes in their spermatogenesis. They are active during the warm breeding season; during this time all stages of spermatogenesis can be seen. However, during the cold non-breeding season, they are inactive, and spermatogenesis is inhibited.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\nRecently there has been a study that shows support that males use chemosensation for the anal glands to locate females in estrous.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The reproductive anatomy of females is most like that of elephants. Female manatees resemble elephants in their zonary placenta, bicornuate uterus, and corpora albicantia. The female manatees\xe2\x80\x99 ovary gross morphology consists of an oval disk shape with a flat surface of the cortex. And since they are polyovular the ovulation scar is often challenging to identify.<sup id="cite_ref-Sheldon_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheldon-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">gestation</a> period in manatees lasts for 12\xe2\x80\x9314 months, after which they give birth to one calf at a time or, rarely, twins.<sup id="cite_ref-NBSIT_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NBSIT-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When twins are born, they are fraternal twins and are dizygotic.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When a calf is born, it usually weighs 60\xe2\x80\x9370&#160;lb (27\xe2\x80\x9332&#160;kg) and is  4.0\xe2\x80\x934.5&#160;ft (1.2\xe2\x80\x931.4&#160;m) long. Manatees do not form permanent <a href="/wiki/Pair_bond" title="Pair bond">pair bonds</a>, and the male contributes no parental care to the calf, which remains with the mother for up to two years prior to weaning. Female manatees have two axillary mammary glands located under their flippers. The lactation period for mothers and their calves last typically for one to two years. Prior to weaning, there is an increase in the hormone progesterone in the mother.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\nDuring this two-year period with their mother, calves learn how to locate warm water sources for winter and about migration routes for the summer seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A single female can reproduce once every 2\xe2\x80\x933 years, which is referred to as the calving interval.<sup id="cite_ref-South_Florida_Multi-1999_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Florida_Multi-1999-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Wild manatees have been documented producing offspring into their late 30s, and a female captive manatee has given birth in her 40s.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Captive breeding programs are not being conducted for the Florida manatee due to conservation concerns that are being addressed in their natural environments. Until these concerns are rectified, a captive breeding program will not be beneficial for this species.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Threats_and_conservation">Threats and conservation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Threats and conservation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:West_Indian_manatee_Paraba_Brazil.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/West_Indian_manatee_Paraba_Brazil.jpg/220px-West_Indian_manatee_Paraba_Brazil.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/West_Indian_manatee_Paraba_Brazil.jpg/330px-West_Indian_manatee_Paraba_Brazil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/West_Indian_manatee_Paraba_Brazil.jpg/440px-West_Indian_manatee_Paraba_Brazil.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a><figcaption>Manatees in a conservation project in Brazilian northeastern coast</figcaption></figure>\n<p>The West Indian manatee has been included on the U.S. Endangered Species List since the 1970s.  In October 2007, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN) assessed the West Indian manatee as vulnerable, and both the Florida and Antillean subspecies as endangered.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The full species and both subspecies qualified for their respective classifications due to a combination of a low number of mature individuals, and a significant projected decline in total population numbers over the next two generations (about 20 years per generation in manatees). Similarly, <a href="/wiki/NatureServe" title="NatureServe">NatureServe</a> considers both the Florida subspecies and the species as a whole as "Imperiled".<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The Florida and Antillean subspecies face different threats, though some overlap. The largest causes of Florida manatee deaths can be attributed to collisions with watercraft, the loss of warm-water habitat, high perinatal mortality, entanglements and ingestion of debris, pollution, habitat loss, and harmful algae blooms (HABs), such as <a href="/wiki/Red_tide" class="mw-redirect" title="Red tide">red tide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USGS2_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Manatee_Mortality_Statistics_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manatee_Mortality_Statistics-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Antillean manatees face severe <a href="/wiki/Habitat_fragmentation" title="Habitat fragmentation">habitat fragmentation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Castelblanco-Mart\xc3\xadnez-2012_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Castelblanco-Mart\xc3\xadnez-2012-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as well as continued pressure from illegal hunting.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In some countries, like Belize, collisions with watercraft are on the rise as tourism grows.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The 2017 decision to reclassify the West Indian manatee from endangered to threatened under the Endangered Species Act cited increases in the populations of both subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-fws_manatee_threatened_201733_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fws_manatee_threatened_201733-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The decision was not without controversy, however: According to <a href="/wiki/Save_the_Manatee_Club" title="Save the Manatee Club">Save the Manatee Club</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Fish_and_Wildlife_Service" title="United States Fish and Wildlife Service">U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service</a> failed to adequately consider data from 2010 to 2016, during which time manatees suffered from unprecedented mortality events linked to habitat pollution, dependence on artificial warm-water sources, and record deaths from watercraft strikes.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  The official notice of the reclassification made clear that, even with the downlisting, all federal protections for the West Indian manatee under the Endangered Species Act would remain in place.<sup id="cite_ref-smithsonianmag_201722_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smithsonianmag_201722-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The West Indian manatee is also protected by the Florida Manatee Sanctuary Act of 1978<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Marine_Mammal_Protection_Act_of_1972" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine Mammal Protection Act of 1972">Marine Mammal Protection Act of 1972</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="T._m._latirostris_(Florida_manatee)"><span id="T._m._latirostris_.28Florida_manatee.29"></span><i>T. m. latirostris</i> (Florida manatee)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: T. m. latirostris (Florida manatee)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>A 1997 population viability analysis of the Florida manatee projected a 44% chance of extinction within the next 1,000 years without improvements in habitat conditions and new protective regulations.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since then, prospects for the subspecies have improved. In 2016, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">U.S. Geological Survey</a> collaborated with the Florida Fish and Wildlife Service to re-evaluate the status of the Florida manatee under the Core Biological Model, which is used to estimate population viability. This assessment analyzed total population on the east and west coasts of Florida and estimated that the likelihood of either population falling under 500 adults within 100 years was about 0.42%.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The projected improvement of population numbers and stability is contingent on continued intervention and monitoring efforts.\n</p>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Manatee_Crossing_Sign.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Manatee_Crossing_Sign.jpg/220px-Manatee_Crossing_Sign.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="191" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Manatee_Crossing_Sign.jpg/330px-Manatee_Crossing_Sign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Manatee_Crossing_Sign.jpg/440px-Manatee_Crossing_Sign.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1771" data-file-height="1535" /></a><figcaption>Manatee Crossing Sign from South Florida Museum</figcaption></figure>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Collision_with_watercraft">Collision with watercraft</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Collision with watercraft"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Over 20% of manatee mortalities per year are a result of watercraft collisions.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Between 1985 and 2017, 53% of adult-manatee mortalities were the result of boat strikes.<sup id="cite_ref-Manatee_Mortality_Statistics_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manatee_Mortality_Statistics-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 96% of adult Florida manatee carcasses have scars from a boat collision with some individuals showing signs of 10 or more boat strikes.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Manatees can hear boats, but they oftentimes do not have enough time to get out of the way of a fast approaching boat. A boat going at a slower speed gives the manatee more time to react and move out of the way.<sup id="cite_ref-MMC_20062_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MMC_20062-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Manatees respond to approaching vessels by orienting towards deeper waters and increasing their speed,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but they are nevertheless frequently struck as they don\'t have the ability to swim very fast and, in shallow habitat, they oftentimes don\'t have anywhere to go to get out of the way. Over half of all watercraft deaths are caused by the impact trauma from the hull of a fast-speeding boat, whereas the remaining percentage is caused by the boat propeller.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Aerial surveys of Florida manatee and boat distribution have been conducted to map the areas in which collisions are most likely to occur, accounting for environmental and seasonal factors.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Watercraft can frequently avoid hitting manatees simply by reducing speed, allowing time for the manatee to escape out of range.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite improvements in modeling and changes to local regulations, manatee mortalities from watercraft collisions continue to rise, with an all-time high of 137 recorded deaths in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Loss_of_warm-water_habitat">Loss of warm-water habitat</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Loss of warm-water habitat"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Projected long-term loss of warm-water habitats presents a significant risk to manatees, which are unable to tolerate temperatures lower than 20&#160;\xc2\xb0C (68&#160;\xc2\xb0F) for prolonged periods of time.<sup id="cite_ref-haubold_20072_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haubold_20072-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-\xe2\x80\x9creep2006\xe2\x80\x9d2_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-\xe2\x80\x9creep2006\xe2\x80\x9d2-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Florida manatees frequently congregate around natural warm-water springs, as well as the warm discharge given off by power plants throughout the winter months. However, as older plants are replaced with more energy-efficient structures, manatees could become at risk of cold-induced death due to reduced availability of warm-water refuges.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Laist-2005_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Laist-2005-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Manatees exhibit high site fidelity and can recall previous refuge sites to which they frequently return in successive winters. Some conservationists fear that manatees may become overly reliant on warm-water locations generated by power plants that may shut down soon.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Natural warm-water springs face problems from over-pumping of groundwater for human consumption, reduced spring flow, and nutrient pollution. In addition, some springs that were historically accessible to manatees are blocked off by dams or other structures.<sup id="cite_ref-Laist-2005_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Laist-2005-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As Florida moves towards net zero by 2050, power companies will likely phase out these warm water discharges.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Florida Climate Institute\xe2\x80\x99s Laying the Groundwork for \xe2\x80\x98Getting to Neutral\xe2\x80\x99 in the State of Florida project sets a foundation for a net-zero action planning effort in Florida by 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In areas where manatees lack access to power plants or natural warm springs, they instead seek out natural deep water zones that passively remain above tolerable temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Harmful_algae_blooms">Harmful algae blooms</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Harmful algae blooms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Harmful algae blooms are an additional threat to manatees.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Red_tide" class="mw-redirect" title="Red tide">Red tide</a> is a common name for algal blooms, deriving its distinctive color from pigmentation molecules in the algae. The blooms produce <a href="/wiki/Brevetoxin" title="Brevetoxin">brevetoxins</a> which are potentially fatal to marine life. Most manatee deaths from red tide occur when manatees inhale the toxins, or ingest the toxins when consuming seagrass, their primary food source.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During a 1996 epizootic more than 150 manatees died from red tide exposure.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although manatees can survive exposure to red tide if rescued and rehabilitated in time, researchers fear that there might be long-term effects from sub-lethal red-tide exposure, which can affect the manatee\'s immune system through reduced lymphocyte proliferation response.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition to red tide, blue/green and brown algae blooms triggered by excessive nutrient pollution from fertilizer, runoff, and leaking septic tanks can cloud the water. This results in seagrasses dying off since seagrasses and aquatic plants need light to photosynthesize. Manatees in turn may not be able to find sufficient food sources, which can lead to starvation.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Manatees have experienced several Unusual Mortality Events (UMEs) over the years, which are usually related to either cold-stress syndrome, or brevetoxicosis from red tide. A current UME is ongoing along the Atlantic coast of Florida and is most likely related to a shortage in food sources.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Entanglement,_entrapment_and_ingestion_of_debris"><span id="Entanglement.2C_entrapment_and_ingestion_of_debris"></span>Entanglement, entrapment and ingestion of debris</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Entanglement, entrapment and ingestion of debris"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Manatees easily can get entangled in monofilament fishing line, crab traps, and other debris, which can lead to the loss of flippers and severe body wraps. In addition, they may also ingest a variety of debris, such as plastics, cloth, fishing gear, and other items.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Manatees can also get crushed by flood control structures and locks and may get entrapped behind weirs or other structures, especially after heavy rainfall. Some manatees have gotten trapped in culverts and pipes and needed intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:West_Indian_manatee_skeletons_NC.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/West_Indian_manatee_skeletons_NC.jpg/220px-West_Indian_manatee_skeletons_NC.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/West_Indian_manatee_skeletons_NC.jpg/330px-West_Indian_manatee_skeletons_NC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/West_Indian_manatee_skeletons_NC.jpg/440px-West_Indian_manatee_skeletons_NC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a><figcaption>West Indian manatee skeletons on display at the <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Museum_of_Natural_Sciences" title="North Carolina Museum of Natural Sciences">North Carolina Museum of Natural Sciences</a> in <a href="/wiki/Raleigh,_North_Carolina" title="Raleigh, North Carolina">Raleigh, North Carolina</a></figcaption></figure>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="T._m._manatus_(Antillean_manatee)"><span id="T._m._manatus_.28Antillean_manatee.29"></span><i>T. m. manatus</i> (Antillean manatee)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=West_Indian_manatee&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: T. m. manatus (Antillean manatee)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>There is relatively little data on the Antillean manatee compared to its Florida counterpart.  Phylogenetic studies on the Antillean manatee have revealed low genetic diversity among its populations, likely due to limitations on dispersal.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Captive breeding, as well as the rehabilitation of manatee calves discovered without their mothers nearby, can supplement local conservation strategies, but populations are nevertheless at risk of inbreeding depression and local extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Whereas the population trends of the Florida manatee are relatively well monitored, population data for the Antillean manatee is sparse due to its patchy distribution, as well as the relative <a href="/wiki/Turbidity" title="Turbidity">turbidity</a> and low levels of light in its habitat which can mitigate the effectiveness of aerial or sonar-based surveys, often resulting in inaccurate or erroneous measurements.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2016 study on the Antillean manatee population of Brazil found a potential range for current population size between 485 and 2,221 individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service estimated in 2017 that as many as 6,782 individuals might exist in the Antillean subspecies, but these results rely in large part on personal anecdotes and may overestimate the true population number.<sup id="cite_ref-fws_manatee_threatened_201733_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fws_manatee_threatened_201733-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2012 study of the Antillean population documented significant human-related causes of mortality, and projected that if the share of human-caused manatee deaths per year rose to 5% or more, the population would face a severe decline and eventual extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-Castelblanco-Mart\xc3\xadnez-2012_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Castelblanco-Mart\xc3\xadnez-2012-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2
